  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer10965109375420713416">Arch-swindler Moist Van Lipwig never believed his confidence crimes were hanging offenses - until he found himself with a noose tightly around his neck, dropping through a trapdoor, and falling into...a government job?<br/><br/>By all rights, Moist should have met his maker.   <span id="freeText10965109375420713416" style="display:none">Arch-swindler Moist Van Lipwig never believed his confidence crimes were hanging offenses - until he found himself with a noose tightly around his neck, dropping through a trapdoor, and falling into...a government job?<br /><br />By all rights, Moist should have met his maker. Instead, it's Lord Vetinari, supreme ruler of Ankh-Morpork, who promptly offers him a job as Postmaster. Since his only other option is a nonliving one, Moist accepts the position - and the hulking golem watchdog who comes along with it, just in case Moist was considering abandoning his responsibilities prematurely. <br /><br />Getting the moribund Postal Service up and running again, however, may be a near-impossible task, what with literally mountains of decades-old undelivered mail clogging every nook and cranny of the broken-down post office building; and with only a few creaky old postmen and one rather unstable, pin-obsessed youth available to deliver it. Worse still, Moist could swear the mail is talking to him. Worst of all, it means taking on the gargantuan, money-hungry Grand Trunk clacks communication monopoly and its bloodthirsty piratical head, Mr. Reacher Gilt.<br />But it says on the building Neither Rain Nor Snow Nor Glom of Nit...Inspiring words (admittedly, some of the bronze letters have been stolen), and for once in his wretched life Moist is going to fight. And if the bold and impossible are what's called for, he'll do it - in order to move the mail, continue breathing, get the girl, and specially deliver that invaluable commodity that every human being (not to mention troll, dwarf, and, yes, even golem) requires: hope.</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think if this was a trend through all of Prachets books this would be a problem. However, in many of his other books, he passes the B test with flyi<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think if this was a trend through all of Prachets books this would be a problem. However, in many of his other books, he passes the B test with flying colours. As others have said, this narrative is focused on a man, and is mostly from his perspective. <br /><br />So whilst it DOESN'T pass the test, because it is not a re-occurring issue with Prachet, I don't think this is something we need to really get too worried about. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/61838006-samantha"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1493552578i/61838006._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/61838006-samantha">Samantha</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Not really. This was the first book I read in the Discworld series and I wasn't lost. Most of these books are standalones or series that all take plac<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Not really. This was the first book I read in the Discworld series and I wasn't lost. Most of these books are standalones or series that all take place in the same universe. It does make occasional references to things that happened in other books, but nothing very important or essential to your understanding of this one. I would really recommend this as a good starting point to get into the series.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_1329987712" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1329987712" />
    <a title="Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1720620-anne"><img alt="Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415374035p2/1720620.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1329987712?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne" href="/user/show/1720620-anne">Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780060824693" href="/book/show/13619116-going-postal">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Anne by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4846819-anna">Anna</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=magic-stuff">magic-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720620-anne?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1329987712" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18281201013001258119"><b>3.5 stars</b><br/><br/>This is only my 2nd <b>Discworld</b> book, so technically, I've skipped over 32 books to get to <b>Going Postal</b>. I didn't care for the 1st book in the series, but this one kept getting recommended to me over and over again, so I thought I'd give the audiobook version a shot.<br/>The first several hours were kind of boring and unfunny (to me) so I ended up giving up on it and listening to a few romance novels and a Sherlock Holmes story before I came back and finished it out.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1598642613ra/30028625.gif' width='400' height='250' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Basically, I ran out of oth</span>
  <span id="freeText18281201013001258119" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars</b><br><br>This is only my 2nd <b>Discworld</b> book, so technically, I've skipped over 32 books to get to <b>Going Postal</b>. I didn't care for the 1st book in the series, but this one kept getting recommended to me over and over again, so I thought I'd give the audiobook version a shot.<br>The first several hours were kind of boring and unfunny (to me) so I ended up giving up on it and listening to a few romance novels and a Sherlock Holmes story before I came back and finished it out.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1598642613ra/30028625.gif" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Basically, I ran out of other stuff to listen to and was forced into it.<br>BUT. <br>Amazingly, the story got a lot better!<br>It wasn't necessarily what I would personally call 'funny' but it <i>was</i> clever. And on the upside, you really don't need to have read any of the other books in the series to understand what is going on. I mean, I'm sure I missed out on a lot of inside jokes, but since I didn't think the regular jokes were particularly hilarious, I'm 100% ok with that.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1598656257ra/30029216.gif" width="350" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The gist is that this lovable con artist gets executed for his crimes...but not really. Lord Vetinari recruits him forcibly into the role of Postmaster. At which point, Moist von Lipwig finds himself saddled with crazy co-workers, shadowed by a golem parole officer, stuck in a rundown building with some sort of reality-warping machine at its core, harassed by corporate villains who are trying to kill him, and almost unconsciously chasing after a strangely sexy golem placement agent/advocate who smokes like a chimney.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1598656098ra/30029211.gif" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I honestly think that once you get past the first 3d of the book, this thing really picks up. The short version is that this sucker is a cautionary tale about corporate monopolies set in a fun alternate universe.<br>The long version is...well, much longer.<br><b>Recommended for fans of Discworld.</b><br><br>Stephen Briggs - Narrator<br>Publisher: HarperAudio<br>Edition: Unabridged<br>Awards: <br>Audie Award Nominee<br>Margaret A. Edwards Award</span>
  <a data-text-id="18281201013001258119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1153186674" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1153186674" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1153186674?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=humorous-fiction">humorous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1153186674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer588932175729240939"><b>The mail must get through!</b><br/><br/><b>He was part of the government, wasn't he? Governments took money off people. That's what they were <i>for</i>.</b><br/><br/>Turns out, years spent scamming and conning the public have excellently prepared Moist von Lipwig with the skill set needed to run a vast corporation.<br/><br/>Who knew?<br/><br/>My Discworld-reading for the Humour Club has had its ups and downs. Some books I loved, some bored me to tears, one I didn't finish. I thrilled to the adventures of the witches, laughed at the exploits of the Nig</span>
  <span id="freeText588932175729240939" style="display:none"><b>The mail must get through!</b><br /><br /><b>He was part of the government, wasn't he? Governments took money off people. That's what they were <i>for</i>.</b><br /><br />Turns out, years spent scamming and conning the public have excellently prepared Moist von Lipwig with the skill set needed to run a vast corporation.<br /><br />Who knew?<br /><br />My Discworld-reading for the Humour Club has had its ups and downs. Some books I loved, some bored me to tears, one I didn't finish. I thrilled to the adventures of the witches, laughed at the exploits of the Night Watch and frowned upon the know-it-all wizards (Seriously, could they be more pompous? It's like reading a book where every single character is my mother-in-law!). Yet, I've never rated anything less than three stars because I laughed, at least once, while reading each book. <br /><br />But now...<br /><br /><b>
  <i>I'm in love again, and the spring is comin'<br />I'm in love again, hear my heart strings strummin'</i>
</b>*<br /><br />This one is EVERYTHING a Discworld book can be - funny, touching, clever and thrilling. If you're looking to start the series, this is a perfect place to jump in. Moist's efforts to revive the floundering postal service should make you grin and require absolutely no knowledge of previous tales. <br /><br />ADDED BONUS - Pratchett has invented possibly <i>the</i> nicest euphemism ever for describing old age - It's good to know I'm not decrepit, I'm just <b>rich in years</b>.<br /><br />A perfect pick-me-up of a read! <br /><br />*<i>Cole Porter</i></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1908518758" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1908518758" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1908518758?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1908518758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14677049503790633492">I love it when one work of art makes me think of another and my enjoyment of both is enhanced.<br/><br/>The executive office meeting subsequent to the gallows made me think of Mel Brooks’ hilarious 1974 film Blazing Saddles and Harvey Korman’s portrayal of Hedley LaMarr. So throughout the rest of the novel I eschewed the British aristocratic drawl of Lord Vetinari and replaced with Korman’s robber baron prose for full comedic effect.<br/><br/>Granny Weatherwax is still my favorite, but Sir Terry has succeeded again</span>
  <span id="freeText14677049503790633492" style="display:none">I love it when one work of art makes me think of another and my enjoyment of both is enhanced.<br><br>The executive office meeting subsequent to the gallows made me think of Mel Brooks’ hilarious 1974 film Blazing Saddles and Harvey Korman’s portrayal of Hedley LaMarr. So throughout the rest of the novel I eschewed the British aristocratic drawl of Lord Vetinari and replaced with Korman’s robber baron prose for full comedic effect.<br><br>Granny Weatherwax is still my favorite, but Sir Terry has succeeded again in making a GREAT Discworld novel that is funny, thought provoking and entertaining all at once.<br><br>While Moist von Lipwig is a worthy and fun protagonist, once again the real hero is Pratchett himself. The author’s pithy prose and world wise narration keeps this skipping along at a good clip and the Pratchett Smile O’ Meter was bouncing in a lively manner throughout.<br><br>Describing a picaresque tale about erstwhile con man and ne’er do-well Lipwig, Pratchett shows how a second chance after the hangman’s noose is a win-win for all involved, especially the readers. Parolee Lipwig must recreate the Ankh-Morpork post office into a going concern. We revisit many of our Discworld favorites, meet some new friends, and a rousing good time is had by all. Besides being a great entry into the Discworld mythos, this is also just a good book; Pratchett, getting better like a fine wine, spins a good yarn.<br><br>One of the best Discworld books, a MUST read for fans.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548179150i/26956025._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="14677049503790633492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552158121" href="/book/show/6771432-going-postal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=saw_the_movie_first">saw_the_movie_first</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=quotable">quotable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=part_of_a_series">part_of_a_series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1407437645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13917102349990974855">For the longest time, <strong>Going Postal</strong> has been my best known <em>unread</em> book. Having watched and <em>re</em>watched the SkyOne TV adaptation, I had sort of tacitly decided that <em>actually</em> reading the book was not high on my list of priorities. Plus, Charles Dance is the quintessential Lord Vetinari as far as I'm concerned. Sorry GoT fans, the Discworld was here long before you.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1584611706ra/29129253.gif' width='270' alt='Charles Dance' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Moist von Lipwig has dedicated most of his life to perfecting the intricacies of con-artistry, but his unparalleled streak of luck is abou</span>
  <span id="freeText13917102349990974855" style="display:none">For the longest time, <strong>Going Postal</strong> has been my best known <em>unread</em> book. Having watched and <em>re</em>watched the SkyOne TV adaptation, I had sort of tacitly decided that <em>actually</em> reading the book was not high on my list of priorities. Plus, Charles Dance is the quintessential Lord Vetinari as far as I'm concerned. Sorry GoT fans, the Discworld was here long before you.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1584611706ra/29129253.gif" width="270" alt="Charles Dance" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Moist von Lipwig has dedicated most of his life to perfecting the intricacies of con-artistry, but his unparalleled streak of luck is about to meet a rather definitive ending. Or is it? Lord Havelock Vetinari is in need of a new Post Master for Ankh-Morpork's long deceased Post Office. A truly impossible job even for Lipwig, but our hero's had plenty of experience in slinking off. Unfortunately for him, the Patrician has had plenty of experience <em>preventing</em> said slinking off.<br><br>And since no request from Lord Vetinari ever comes with just <em>one</em> catch, Lipwig soon realizes that any possible failure may have some truly fatal consequences. And that's not just due to Vetinari's retaliation.<br><br>So with no other alternatives, and a parole officer immune to any forms of bribery, Lipwig decides to just... fake his way through the Post Master job. And against all odds, our hero becomes an instant success.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1584611706ra/29129254.gif" alt="Postal workers' dance" width="270" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So how was the reading experience, you may ask? As predictable as I suspected, but also... not that much. The generic idea remains, of Lipwig managing to surround himself with some of the most capable people in order to achieve his goals, especially when said people had no idea of their own capabilities. Except for Ms. Dearheart of course, who should just be declared a Goddess, so we can all bow down to her <em>officially</em>.<br><br><blockquote>
<br>“What kind of man would put a known criminal in charge of a major branch of government? Apart from, say, the average voter.”<br><br>“That was an important rule of any game: always make it easy for people to give you money.”<br><br>“And you just hoped something would turn up? What made you think it would?"<br>"It always has. The only way to get something to turn up when you need it is to need it to turn up.”<br>
</blockquote><br><br><em>So which one is better?</em> Honestly, I liked both versions.<br><br><strong>The TV adaptation</strong> has turned Adora Dearheart into this absolutely kickass heroine, who doesn't just command the Golem Trust, but also dabbles in Clacks Tower experiments, tames crazy horses, and is generally up for saving the World any time and any where.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1584884753i/29147266.png" alt="Adorabelle Dearheart" width="270" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><strong>The book</strong> on the other hand gives you awesome bonus scenes like:<br>- Moist getting inducted into the secret Postal Workers' cult<br>- Miss Macalariat the ferocious office manager of the Post Office, intent on proper bathroom etiquette even when golems where involved.<br>- and of course Grandad Lipwig's many teachings back in Uberwald, the building blocks of Moist's survival strategies.<br><br>All I can suggest is not to reread/rewatch either so much, as to be able to anticipate each and every twist and turn. It'll take away from the excitement of discovering Lipwigs next crazy scheme.<br><br><strong>Score: 3.8/5 stars</strong><br><br>Surprisingly entertaining, in spite of all my initial misgivings.<br><br>======================<br>Other books starring Moist von Lipwig:<br>Review of book 2: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/435878758" rel="nofollow noopener">Making Money</a><br>Review of book 3: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2073459878" rel="nofollow noopener">Raising Steam</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13917102349990974855" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3110899373" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3110899373" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3110899373?book_show_action=true">Feb 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/43670856-going-postal">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3110899373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11735814993365278272">Welcome to the Industrial Revolution! Well, technically we already had three books belonging to that sub-series (though I only really consider two of those truly to be of that subject), but this one is full of ... industry. And not as in factories but as in a man's industrious efforts.<br/><br/>Meet Moist von Lipwick. No, his parents really didn't think it through when naming him. He is up to no good, cheating people out of money (though always without using any form of violence) and he is about to pay th</span>
  <span id="freeText11735814993365278272" style="display:none">Welcome to the Industrial Revolution! Well, technically we already had three books belonging to that sub-series (though I only really consider two of those truly to be of that subject), but this one is full of ... industry. And not as in factories but as in a man's industrious efforts.<br><br>Meet Moist von Lipwick. No, his parents really didn't think it through when naming him. He is up to no good, cheating people out of money (though always without using any form of violence) and he is about to pay the price at the beginning of this book. Because Lord Vetinari has ordered his death. So he's hanged ... until an inch of his life ... literally.<br>(I really love the Patrician.)<br>Afterwards, he has an audience with Vetinari where he is voluntold to be the next Postmaster of Ankh-Morpork. You see, the industry (there is that word again) that has formed around the Clacks towers is getting out of hand, the moguls becoming almost unruly, which is something the Patrician certainly cannot stand. However, previous attempts at reinstating a postmaster and re-starting the regular delivery of mail for some much needed competition have all ... failed. It takes a swindler to get ahead of the crooks (incl. their pirate boss).<br>So Lipwick gets a very special parol officer, Ankh-Morpork gets stamps and mailmen, and the letters that have waited decades to be delivered finally get to rest.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583013891i/29038821._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Technically, there is no reason for me to like a stinking big metropolis like Ankh-Morpork. And yet ... every time I read a book that takes place there, I'm loving every minute of it. Everyone trying to get one over the citizens there deserves what he's going to get. *lol*<br>I also love the diversity. This time represented by the wonderful golem Mr. Pump (Pump 19).<br><br>And how could anyone not love the fact that this installment of the series centers around stories and unfinished life stories at that! Personally, I love writing and sending and receiving letters so reading an entire book that was crafted around the notion that mail means communication and connection and HOPE, was rather magical indeed!<br><br>The writing is top notch, there is no denying it and no need to go over all the glory that is Pratchett's mastery. Just look at the highlighted quotes or read almost any of my other reviews for more on that. But his newest character addition, Moist, is a masterpiece! I love a number of characters in the series but this <s>confidence trickster</s> hero is something else! I'm glad to know there will be more books with him in the not so distant future and I'm very much looking forward to his continued shenanigans.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11735814993365278272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41913379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18135028522322376271">This book was an excellent surprise; I love the Discworld series but was getting tired of seeing the same characters again and again in every book. But this book introduces an entirely new and refreshing cast of characters. I particularly liked the main character and felt he was very well-developed. <br/><br/>Pratchett's books are funny precisely because he has a very firm grasp of human nature. His best work feels surprisingly profound even though you're also laughing so hard you're afraid you might brea</span>
  <span id="freeText18135028522322376271" style="display:none">This book was an excellent surprise; I love the Discworld series but was getting tired of seeing the same characters again and again in every book. But this book introduces an entirely new and refreshing cast of characters. I particularly liked the main character and felt he was very well-developed. <br /><br />Pratchett's books are funny precisely because he has a very firm grasp of human nature. His best work feels surprisingly profound even though you're also laughing so hard you're afraid you might break a rib. In some of the later novels I was getting more of a reused-joke feeling, and while it was still pretty funny, all the depth was gone. In this novel it's back, and I would rank <i>Going Postal</i> pretty high among the Discworld novels.<br /><br />Highly recommended, especially to Pratchett fans who think Discworld has gotten a little stale. </span>

  <span id="freeText1028191930817934110" style="display:none">Out of all the books in the Discworld series, this is the first one that made me truly go from a fan to a diehard fan. I enjoyed them all, to be sure, but this is the one that made me downright giddy and pleased.<br /><br />It’s a heist!! Um. Kinda. When a heist meets becoming an avatar for a god of incomplete stories meets confidence trickery meets MAIL DELIVERY.<br /><br />I mean, this is Discworld. It makes perfect sense. Who other than a successful (albeit caught by Lord V) thief to run the biggest government con job in Discworld?<br /><br />All hail the Postmaster General. Beloved of all the gods and the crookedest personality in all of Pratchett’s works. :)<br /><br /> So funny, so magical, and so freakishly... real.<br /><br />If you don’t steal enough, you’re hung as a thief.           <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36434323-melindam?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36434323-melindam?shelf=fantasy-by-male-authors">fantasy-by-male-authors</a>,

<div id="review_34877185" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34877185" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34877185?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060013134" href="/book/show/34539.Going_Postal">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who refuse to read fantasy novels.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2008">read-in-2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=britain">britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34877185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4938795733707047313">This was really surprising, actually. Perhaps it shouldn't have been, given how many people whose opinions I respect are Pratchett fans, but I haven't read a fantasy novel since giving up on Robert Jordan's painstakingly prolonged <i>The Wheel of Time</i> (somewhere in book 5, I think) long before college. Actually, I suppose I read a few of the Harry Potters in the interim, but in some ways that felt more like keeping up with pop culture than reading actual fantasy.<br/><br/>In any event, <i>Going Postal</i> was purel</span>
  <span id="freeText4938795733707047313" style="display:none">This was really surprising, actually. Perhaps it shouldn't have been, given how many people whose opinions I respect are Pratchett fans, but I haven't read a fantasy novel since giving up on Robert Jordan's painstakingly prolonged <i>The Wheel of Time</i> (somewhere in book 5, I think) long before college. Actually, I suppose I read a few of the Harry Potters in the interim, but in some ways that felt more like keeping up with pop culture than reading actual fantasy.<br /><br />In any event, <i>Going Postal</i> was purely a pleasure. Compared to the grueling epics I recall from years ago, Pratchett's style was brisk and entertaining, his humor completely suffusing the tone of the book in a way that reminded me of Douglas' Adams' approach to science fiction. But whereas Adams' plotlines can be meandering and perhaps secondary to his constant seeking of the absurd, Pratchett's were elaborately coiled, well-paced, and compelling, and his characters, amusing and strange as they often were, had a certain sincerity that kept me interested in their welfare. Ridiculous name aside, Moist von Lipwig's reluctantly scrupulous con artist (emphasis on 'artist', as his maneuvering definitely deserves such a word) was exceedingly entertaining to follow.<br /><br />Pratchett's underlying thoughts and satire also seemed fairly spot-on. His commentary on the nature of hope suggests that he would understand why so many under-privileged Americans tend to vote Republican against their best financial interests (besides its application in many other areas of human nature), and the bits about personal momentum were sufficiently commanding as to grant even the reader a resounding sense of motion and possibility from time to time.<br /><br />I suppose Pratchett may not be to everyone's taste (and I was not sure he would be to mine -- fantasy comedy, never!) but he seems to be very skilled in his particular niche. Yes, this was very good. Mostly just at being a fun diversion, but also perhaps as well thought out and gracefully executed literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4938795733707047313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1699657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14046734037656808453">I picked up this book to read on a 15 hour flight from Chicago to Vietnam, on the assumption that it would be a light, pleasant way to pass the dreary hours (after one flight to China during which I watched the first X-Men film three times, I decided it was best to err on the side of having too many books rather than too few). <br/><br/>My assumptions proved correct, and I enjoyed the book quite thoroughly. Three things struck me:<br/><br/>1) Pratchett's use of language is always fun, and he occasionally makes wond</span>
  <span id="freeText14046734037656808453" style="display:none">I picked up this book to read on a 15 hour flight from Chicago to Vietnam, on the assumption that it would be a light, pleasant way to pass the dreary hours (after one flight to China during which I watched the first X-Men film three times, I decided it was best to err on the side of having too many books rather than too few). <br /><br />My assumptions proved correct, and I enjoyed the book quite thoroughly. Three things struck me:<br /><br />1) Pratchett's use of language is always fun, and he occasionally makes wonderful jokes that can't be read aloud with the same effect, like the store owner who turns up whose speech suffers from greengrocer's apostrophes.<br /><br />2) Pratchett typically writes in a third person limited point of view, but he's extremely good at getting inside the heads of his characters and changing the voice up. The unfortunately named Moist has his own clear-eyed perspective on the world that differs from the other denizens of Discworld.<br /><br />3) He manages to weave in quite a bit of history about the postal service. I wouldn't have considered it to be an interesting topic for inquiry (I mean, come on; post offices mostly exist to aggrivate people who hate standing in queues, right?) but I was wrong.<br /><br />Best vocabulary word gleaned from the text: "frank", meaning to stamp something.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14046734037656808453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText1944315927785523965" style="display:none">I am probably the last person on earth to read Terry Pratchett, but I'm glad I finally did. <div id="review_3179709839" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3179709839" />
    <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud"><img alt="Oleksandr Zholud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506182069p2/4743119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3179709839?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oleksandr Zholud" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud">Oleksandr Zholud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781407032474" href="/book/show/45154702-going-postal">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3179709839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10969809432553953790">This is a humorous fantasy novel, part of Discworld series, but can be read as a standalone. It was nominated for Nebula in 2005. I read is as a part of monthly reading in February 2020 at <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hugo & Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group.<br/><br/>“You need a thief to catch a thief”, an old maxim, which both describes the idea behind a story and may be proven wrong at the same time. Moist von Lipwig is a con artist, is about to be hung. He is ‘saved’ by a local enlightened dictator Vetinari and receives a proposal</span>
  <span id="freeText10969809432553953790" style="display:none">This is a humorous fantasy novel, part of Discworld series, but can be read as a standalone. It was nominated for Nebula in 2005. I read is as a part of monthly reading in February 2020 at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels" rel="nofollow noopener">Hugo &amp; Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group.<br /><br />“You need a thief to catch a thief”, an old maxim, which both describes the idea behind a story and may be proven wrong at the same time. Moist von Lipwig is a con artist, is about to be hung. He is ‘saved’ by a local enlightened dictator Vetinari and receives a proposal, which one may refuse, but not live long afterward: to head a local post office. The post is in ruins, both due to negligence and competition: there is a network of semaphore towers, which transmit messages much faster. However, a cabal of greedy bankers now own the network after a hostile acquisition and run it carelessly ‘till it drops’. There are clear allusions to both ‘white elephants’ of public property and businessmen interested only in the short-term gains.<br /><br />Moist is accompanied by bodyguard/parole officer Mr. Pump, a golem. A parole officer is required because Moist tries to run away. A bodyguard is needed because several previous heads of the post met an untimely death.<br /><br />The book is written in tongue-in-cheek style with a lot of references and allusions to other SF and fantasy works: e.g. golems follow Asimov’s laws of robotics, semaphore network has its own hackers called crackers, there are avatars and magic…<br /><br />A nice read but I cannot say I can read a lot of such books in a row, it is a nice palate cleanser but you need to eat something before and after you cleanse your patale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10969809432553953790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115787177" />
    <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova"><img alt="Jeannette Nikolova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480677087p2/2574284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115787177?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeannette Nikolova" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova">Jeannette Nikolova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115787177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12233982120593242605"><a href='https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2017/01/25/going-postal-discworld-33-by-terry-pratchett/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br/><br/><strong>Going Postal</strong> definitely ranks among my top 3 <em>
  <strong>Discworld books.</strong>
</em> That is to say, <strong>Equal Rites</strong> will ALWAYS be my favourite, as it was my first, and the one that got me into <em>Terry Pratchett</em> to begin with. After that, the list gets blurrier, but nevertheless, <strong>Going Postal</strong> is among best.<br/><br/>I saw the <strong>Going Postal</strong> movie no fewer than 3 years ago and I loved it. Which was my reason to postpone reading this book after I suffered from a severe Terry Pratchett fat</span>
  <span id="freeText12233982120593242605" style="display:none"><a href="https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2017/01/25/going-postal-discworld-33-by-terry-pratchett/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br /><br /><strong>Going Postal</strong> definitely ranks among my top 3 <em>
  <strong>Discworld books.</strong>
</em> That is to say, <strong>Equal Rites</strong> will ALWAYS be my favourite, as it was my first, and the one that got me into <em>Terry Pratchett</em> to begin with. After that, the list gets blurrier, but nevertheless, <strong>Going Postal</strong> is among best.<br /><br />I saw the <strong>Going Postal</strong> movie no fewer than 3 years ago and I loved it. Which was my reason to postpone reading this book after I suffered from a severe Terry Pratchett fatigue. Now I'm back, and I'm happy to be here.<br /><br /><strong>Going Postal</strong> is essentially a book about hope. Whatever else is happening in this book, it's actually a wide frame of the story of hope.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"And this was known as the greatest of treasures, which is Hope. It was a good way of getting poorer really very quickly, and staying poor. It could be you. But it wouldn't be."</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />And then later in the book:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"Welcome to fear, said Moist to himself. It's hope, turned inside out. You know you can't go wrong. You're sure it can't go wrong... </em>
  <br />
  <br />
  <em>But it might."</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><em>Moist</em>, as a character, is a personification of the efforts to be <em>good</em>. Or to be <em>better</em>. The struggle against the easy way out, which is to just take what you want (and not even need), as opposed to struggling and fighting for what's worth it. I found Moist mightily gratifying to read about. He was just my type of a main character, crooked and wicked, but motivated to achieve more; aware of his flaws, morally ambiguous, willing to take a wild chance; Attempting to do the impossible, because where is the challenge, otherwise?<br /><br />I liked the secondary characters a lot too. They were all cleverly constructed and very interesting. I know that many would say that this is always the case with Terry Pratchett, but I have grown to feel a gap between his books. As in, same author, same world, and yet there are ones that I loved, like <strong>Going Postal</strong>, and ones that I really, truly hated. And usually, I can set the difference with the witches (whom I adore) and everyone else, most of all the guards, who don't interest me in the slightest. However, in <strong>Going Postal</strong> the characters are definitely not magical, but are awesome nevertheless.<br /><br />I also found something else I was afraid I would never experience again: marveling at Pratchett's cleverness. It's just that whenever I dislike the story, I fail to pay attention to the details, and while this is a character flaw, I feel that it is more or less justified with the idea that you can't force yourself to like something, and the more you dislike it, the more you dislike everything about it.<br /><br /><strong>Going Postal</strong> has given me a new push into the <em>Discworld</em>, and I'm excited to read to the end of the series sometime in the not-so-distant future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12233982120593242605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_144855862" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius"><img alt="Deborah Ideiosepius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425943228p2/4802663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/144855862?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Ideiosepius" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius">Deborah Ideiosepius</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552149433" href="/book/show/395949.Going_Postal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer144855862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8021925872535948242">In this rich complex and darkly humorous story Terry Pratchett takes us to a side of Anke Morpork that the disc-world series had not previously visited; it's post office. <br/><br/>Moist von Lipwig is a con man of major proportions whose last fraud has come to haunt him, caught and about to hang he believes it is all over for him. Saved from permanent death, the Patrician of Anke Morpork, lord Vetinari offers him a job; to revitalise the wrecked and useless Post Office. To do so Moist must use all his ski</span>
  <span id="freeText8021925872535948242" style="display:none">In this rich complex and darkly humorous story Terry Pratchett takes us to a side of Anke Morpork that the disc-world series had not previously visited; it's post office. <br /><br />Moist von Lipwig is a con man of major proportions whose last fraud has come to haunt him, caught and about to hang he believes it is all over for him. Saved from permanent death, the Patrician of Anke Morpork, lord Vetinari offers him a job; to revitalise the wrecked and useless Post Office. To do so Moist must use all his skills as a con artist and many other skills he probably never thought he had.<br /><br />This is a later discworld story, and while I loved the earlier ones when I first came across them, in these later series I think PTerry's writing really came of age. If the early ones are young slightly fizzy wine, then this is a mature dark red, ages for years in oak and with aftertastes that last for hours.<br /><br />Pratchett's distictive brand of social commentary and persistent trend of poking fun at humanity are strong in Going Postal but they do not overwhelm the story, which I think is a perfect page turner. The suite of characters is sizable and well developed, but never out of control so one always knows who is who. Also, I believe that this book could very well be read as a stand alone without losing much: There are many well known characters of the discworld that briefly appear or are mentioned but most of the active characters are new and developed within this story. The exception to this would be Lord Vetinari, whose omnipotence is the cumulative layering of many, many books worth, he has layers, like oysters possibly pearls, or other molluscs, if you will forgive me a little in-joke.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8021925872535948242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer4411375518405087227">8.5/10<br/><br/>My first real attempt at a Terry Pratchett novel. Quite a while ago I picked up Colour of Magic and read it intermittently without ever really getting into it or thinking it was anything too special. I was given Going Postal as a gift a couple of years ago and finally picked it up and attempted it due to my keenness to get into the Discworld series. I'm glad I did!<br/><br/>It's very different to the normal sort of crime/action books I read and I'm trying to broaden my horizons with different styles</span>
  <span id="freeText4411375518405087227" style="display:none">8.5/10<br /><br />My first real attempt at a Terry Pratchett novel. Quite a while ago I picked up Colour of Magic and read it intermittently without ever really getting into it or thinking it was anything too special. I was given Going Postal as a gift a couple of years ago and finally picked it up and attempted it due to my keenness to get into the Discworld series. I'm glad I did!<br /><br />It's very different to the normal sort of crime/action books I read and I'm trying to broaden my horizons with different styles. The first few pages were a bit tricky for me to get into and intially put me off but after a good session I couldn't stop thinking about it. The characters are great with some witty dialogue and scenarios and some of the set-pieces where great. I was hooked at the end and really wanted to find out what happened and how things were going to play out. I read it in a couple of days and for me that is a good effort!<br /><br />This won't be my last outing in the Discworld series and I'm keen to see how Moist Von Lipwig (worst name ever?) fares in his next outing but I'm probably going to go down the "Death" series line next, starting with Mort. The whole idea of Death getting a recruit/apprentice made me want to try Going Postal to see if I could get into the series/style more. It worked and I'm eagerly looking forward to getting Mort.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10516939648694087135">What happens when Ventari becomes an angel and puts a con man in charge of the decrepit Post Office?   <span id="freeText10516939648694087135" style="display:none">What happens when Ventari becomes an angel and puts a con man in charge of the decrepit Post Office? Nothing one expects, of course. <br />--------------------------------------------<br /><br />There was a silence. In that silence, Moist tried out a variety of responses, from "Pull the other one, it's got bells on" to "That's impossible", and decided they all sounded stupid. Groat looked deadly serious, so instead he said: "How?"<br />----------------------------------------------<br /><br />There are many, many characters in the Discworld that I adore. And it is very hard to read the Discworld series without being at least fascinated by Vetinari (as evidenced by the "Vetinari for president!" stickers I often see around). But once in awhile, Pratchett likes to remind people that while Vetinari, while he has style, is, well, a dick.<br /><br />"While this is happening, moreover, no person concerned with the Grand Trunk Company or any of its employees is to leave this room."<br />Greenyham: "You can't do that!"<br />Vetinari: "Can I not? I am a tyrant. It's what we do."<br /><br />(If anyone else likes Vetinari, <i>Guards, Guards</i> is rather essential reading, since it gives the reader some pretty Important Information on how Vetinari go to where he is now.)<br /><br /></span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=mystery-crime-police">mystery-crime-police</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=humorous">humorous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=magic-fantasy">magic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer460623525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer851865468619714609">I have only read the Discworld books featuring the City Watch, and the related follow-up books featuring a spin-off character in this book, Moist Von Lipwig. That's because I'm afraid Pratchett's other books will not be my cuppa. As a writer, he walks a fine line, approaching my personal barrier — too much punnery and parody. I like the City Watch series (see <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_' title='Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Guards! Guards!</a>) because Pratchett toes the line, proving parody in a neatly plotted mystery with the feeling of film noir, and I love the</span>
  <span id="freeText851865468619714609" style="display:none">I have only read the Discworld books featuring the City Watch, and the related follow-up books featuring a spin-off character in this book, Moist Von Lipwig. That's because I'm afraid Pratchett's other books will not be my cuppa. As a writer, he walks a fine line, approaching my personal barrier — too much punnery and parody. I like the City Watch series (see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_" title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a>) because Pratchett toes the line, proving parody in a neatly plotted mystery with the feeling of film noir, and I love the rich character development in Sam Vimes. For the same reasons, I liked this book, Going Postal. It's got a good mix. I enjoy Pratchett's brand of satire, but sometimes the narrative gets a bit preachy and/or teachy. Also, sometimes there are so many puns and parodies that the plot becomes secondary, momentarily. Some allusions and references are unknown to me. These are all minor quibbles and didn't detract from this book. Much.<br /><br />In fact, having said all that, I loved this book! It's fantastical suspense, with humans and golums mainly, but also werewolves, dwarves, trolls, etc. GOING POSTAL is a joy on several counts, but especially character development, plot, humor, and historic references (it's set in a guild-controlled city much like Hamburg or London, around 1400 -- a fictional city, on a flat magical planet called Discworld). The suspense is primarily wrapped around the clacks towers, semaphores, telegraphs. Someone is up to no good on the Grand Trunk.<br /><br />The anti-hero is Mr. Albert Spangler (aka Moist Von Lipwig). Spangler is an expert con-man from Uberwald, a clever operator with a fairly good heart. He is about to be executed for theft when Lord Vetinari, the city dictator (goes by the title Patrician), offers the charming swindler a way out. Will he take it? Will he succeed at setting up a working postal system -- despite ignorance, interference, and outright sabotage? <br /><br />Here is Lord Vetinari, offering Spangler a new lease on life: <blockquote>Albert Spangler is dead, Mr. Lipwig. Three hundred people would swear they saw him die." He leaned forward. "And so, appropriately, it is of angels I wish to talk to you now." <br /><br />Moist managed a grunt. <br /><br />"The first interesting thing about angels, Mr. Lipwig, is that sometimes, very rarely, at a point in a man's career where he has made such a foul and tangled mess of his life that death appears to be the only sensible option, an angel appears to him, or, 1 should say, unto him, and offers him a chance to go back to the moment when it all went wrong, and this time do it right. Mr. Lipwig, I should like you to think of me as ... an angel." </blockquote><br /><br />This is the first book in the Moist Von Lipwig series, which is linked to the City Watch series, which is a stand-alone branch of the Discworld mega series. Not a romance series, but there are some love interests. Moist meets his match, for example. <br /><br />There are several books in the Moist Von Lipvig sub-series and 8 books in the preceding City Watch series -- not all 39 books of the Discworld series apply, many being for children or YA. These two sub-series actually begin with the book Guards! Guards! but one could just start with Going Postal, where we first meet Moist.<br /><br />See my review of Guards! Guards! (A good starting place):<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/460623616">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /><br /><b>
  <u>The Series:</u>
</b> In reading order, books featuring Sam Vimes (CW = City Watch series)<p>Guards! Guards! - 1989 CW<br />Theatre of Cruelty - 1993, CW (4 pages, free, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.lspace.org/books/toc/toc-english.html">http://www.lspace.org/books/toc/toc-e...</a>) <br />Men At Arms - 1993 CW<br />Feet of Clay - 1996 CW<br />Jingo - 1997. CW<br />The Fifth Elephant - 1999 CW<br />The Truth - 2000 (minor character)<br />Night Watch - 2002 CW<br />Monstrous Regiment - 2003 (minor character)<br />Thud! - 2005 CW (and children's book <i>Where's My Cow?)</i><br />Unseen Academicals - 2009 (minor character)<br />Snuff - 2011 CW<br />Raising Steam - 2013 (supporting character)<br /></p><b>(City Watch book links at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/106221-discworld---ankh-morpork-city-watch">https://www.goodreads.com/series/1062...</a>)</b><br /><br /><b>Related "Modernization" (some say "Industrialization") books:</b> There are some spin-off books that cross-over with the "City Watch" sub-series. These books introduce some modernization to the twin cities of Ankh-Morpork. The books feature <b>con-man turned hero, Moist Von Lipvig, </b> and a few characters from the City Watch. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64222.Going_Postal__Discworld___33__Moist_von_Lipwig___1_" title="Going Postal (Discworld, #33; Moist von Lipwig, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Going Postal</a> describes the invention and proliferation of postage stamps (loved it) and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116296.Making_Money__Discworld___36_" title="Making Money (Discworld, #36) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Making Money</a>, depicts the creation of paper money to replace gold (also very good). Then there is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11275323.Raising_Steam__Discworld___40_" title="Raising Steam (Discworld, #40) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Raising Steam</a>, where Lord Vetinari makes Moist Von Lipvig ensure that the newly invented locomotive is profitable and safe for the City (this is the last book written for Discworld).<br /><br />Also, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567670.The_Truth__Discworld___25_" title="The Truth (Discworld, #25) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Truth</a> depicts the invention of a printing press and the subsequent introduction of widespread newspapers into society. Satire deals with the wholesale acceptance of lies when ink-validated. William de Worde (he-he) is the city's first investigative journalist, sleuthing out possible murder and a plot to overthrow Lord Vetinari. This book does not feature Moist Von Lipvig, but it has some City Watch characters. <br /><br />Finally, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a> introduces the film industry to the eager citizens of Ankh-Morpork. This book does not include Moist Von Lipvig. I didn't care as much for it, but it's brimming with satire and some scenes are great.<br /><br /><b>!!! This chart is great for finding your way around the sub-series within the multitude of Discworld novels:<br /> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://i.kinja-img.com/gawker-media/image/upload/s--nZhqj1Uo--/c_fit,fl_progressive,q_80,w_636/695333150797533991.jpg">http://i.kinja-img.com/gawker-media/i...</a></b></span>

<div id="review_146622319" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146622319" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146622319?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=secondary-world">secondary-world</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146622319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6095540891435520238">Okay, so this wasn't brilliant or incredibly original or anything that a 5-star novel should normally be, but it was funny and fast-paced and hugely entertaining. Pratchett's Discworld, with its hodge-podge of technologies, cultures, and magic, into which he shoehorns whatever anachronistic notion works for the story, shouldn't work as well as it does, but somehow you suspend your disbelief and just enjoy the ride.<br/><br/>Starting with the cringeingly-named protagonist "Moist von Lipwig," <i>Going Postal</i> i</span>
  <span id="freeText6095540891435520238" style="display:none">Okay, so this wasn't brilliant or incredibly original or anything that a 5-star novel should normally be, but it was funny and fast-paced and hugely entertaining. Pratchett's Discworld, with its hodge-podge of technologies, cultures, and magic, into which he shoehorns whatever anachronistic notion works for the story, shouldn't work as well as it does, but somehow you suspend your disbelief and just enjoy the ride.<br /><br />Starting with the cringeingly-named protagonist "Moist von Lipwig," <i>Going Postal</i> is a farce with a heart. Lipwig is drafted into running Ankh-Morpork's moribund Post Office, finds himself in competition with the evil Grand Trunk clacks company, and uses all of his evil, swindling, scheming tricks to do good, quite against his will.<br /><br />The book takes shots at government employees, private corporations, finance, hackers, university politics, and all sorts of other things, all in the guise of being a humorous fantasy novel. I'm not usually a big fan of humor, though I am a fan of satire, and I hate puns, but Pratchett's puns actually made me laugh. So do his clever wordplays, even when he's being self-indulgent. And underneath the satire and the tongue-in-cheek worldbuilding, it's a good old-fashioned "bad guy makes good, saves the day, and gets the girl" romance. Highly recommended as a good, fun, light read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6095540891435520238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_137122966" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137122966" />
    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/163750-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1189653481p2/163750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137122966?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/163750-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/163750-nicole?shelf=con-artists">con-artists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/163750-nicole?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/163750-nicole?shelf=fantasy-adult">fantasy-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137122966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10373754087752574656">The length of time it took to read this book is not a reflection upon its quality. I just haven’t been very good at switching between books lately due to a number of distractions.<br/>This one includes two things I have soft spots for: a talented con artist and the postal service. <br/>As I’ve commented in other reviews, such as <i>The Lies of Locke Lamora</i>, I’m fascinated by a well-executed con. Moist von Lipwig (one of Pratchett’s most deliberately awful character names) is one grade-A con artist. He brilli</span>
  <span id="freeText10373754087752574656" style="display:none">The length of time it took to read this book is not a reflection upon its quality. I just haven’t been very good at switching between books lately due to a number of distractions.<br />This one includes two things I have soft spots for: a talented con artist and the postal service. <br />As I’ve commented in other reviews, such as <i>The Lies of Locke Lamora</i>, I’m fascinated by a well-executed con. Moist von Lipwig (one of Pratchett’s most deliberately awful character names) is one grade-A con artist. He brilliantly plays people as if he were a virtuoso and they were violins. But he isn’t, after all, a heartless bastard, which makes him someone to root for. Although Lord Vetinari, the tyrant of Ankh-Morpork, is also a con artist in his own way. The man is a genius, several moves ahead of his opponents; and while he may be ruthless in some ways, I also have to admire him.<br />While I use e-mail and sometimes text messages, I still enjoy writing and receiving old-fashioned paper letters. There’s a personal element to a letter that electronic communication lacks. It makes me sad that a large portion of the current population under 20 years old has never handled a postage stamp. Anyway… The plot hinges on Moist being forced by Vetinari to take over the post office, which has fallen into disrepair, in part due to a new way of sending messages called the clacks. The clacks uses towers and a type of semaphore and light method of passing along messages; the description of the clacks towers at night struck me as quite beautiful. The clacks service is also suffering from problems, due to its unethical owners. Pratchett works his narrative magic yet again, managing to satirise and also humanise. While there is a rivalry between the post office and the clacks, neither is held above the other in the end; both serve a purpose, both employ good people. I won’t get into the intricacies of the plot. Elements of it are familiar, but there are some wonderful twists and turns. <br />There is also a satire of fanatical collecting, starting with pins--and represented by young postal employee Stanley--and turning to stamps. <br />I’d be remiss if I didn’t mention the young woman who catches Moist’s eye. If he has one of the most cringeworthy character names in Discworld, she has one of the cutesiest--Adora Belle Dearheart. Of course, this being Pratchett, she is smart and steely and embittered, chain-smokes, and works for an enterprise that hires out golems. She has a particularly fun scene involving use of her high-heeled shoe to deal with an overly-friendly drunk.<br />Definitely worth reading, whether you’re already a Pratchett fan who just hasn’t gotten to it yet or someone who’s never tried any of his other works. It works well as a stand-alone despite its connections to other Discworld books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10373754087752574656" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
